TITLE: Why Debit Cards Are a Scam
VIDEO_ID: jdlN7WIleso
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jdlN7WIleso
DATE: 2025-09-30
================================================================================

What is small, plastic, and awful for you in an emergency? It's not candy. It's your debit card. Debit cards are one of the worst financial tools that are available to you in the current market. They essentially function very similar to credit cards, but have none of the benefits and all of the downsides. When you go to a store and you swipe a debit card, what ends up happening is your money from your personal bank account is debited instantly. In theory, right, seems fine. If I hand someone cash, it's the same thing. But when you swipe a credit card, it's actually not your money at first that is being paid to the merchant. It is the bank's money, not yours. And there's a fundamental difference here. And the best way to think about it, one is when you're walking around with a debit card, in one sense, you're walking around with however much checking balance you have associated with that card. Yes, of course there are like limitations, but if you were walking around with a lot of $5,000 in cash, you would probably feel a little uneasy. And it kind of is in a sense similar with a debit card because if I go to a store with a debit card and let's say someone found it and they committed fraud on my card, my money is locked up until the bank resolves the dispute. Now, in theory, right, the dispute will be resolved in your favor, but the funds are already pulled from your account. So, let's say I only had a few hundred in my checking account, maybe a few thousand. That money was pulled, that money was taken aside, and then what happens is someone goes and like uses it. Well, great. Now, what happens is I have no money. I have no access to those funds. Even if I'm like, "This is 100% fraud. This wasn't me." Until that dispute is resolved, you don't have access to the funds. A credit card is entirely different because since it is not your money being pulled, the bank can't charge you anything for that fraud fraudulent purchase until it is the case that that dispute has been resolved. And of course, if it's resolved in your favor, nothing's going to be charged. If it resolved against you, then yes, you may be charged something, but that's like, you know, only if it really wasn't fraud. And so what happens is it's an extremely dangerous tool to be carrying around with you because you just have no perks. Your money is gone. It's essentially tied to your account. That's why credit cards are such a much better tool because they have this layer of protection. And beyond just this general like security aspect of it and the fact that debit cards are just not secure at all compared to credit cards for the consumer, um there's just no additional benefits, right? debit cards because I believe it's the DoddFrank Act, they are capped on their interchange fees. So, banks are not allowed to provide rewards. I mean, aren't really economically able to provide rewards to people using them. The exceptions are some small NEO banks because of the way the law is written. If your bank holds a certain amount of assets um or below a certain amount of assets, you are allowed to charge a small interchange fee, which is why there are some rewards debit cards out there. Uh but they are much less common than credit cards. And so, this means really you have no perks. um you know, credit cards, especially on the premium side, you'll have like purchase protections, extended warranty, return protections, points earning, which is obviously is a huge difference in the rebates, and that you're literally just like wasting like 1 to 3% of your money if you're not using a credit card. Um, and so it's it's this big differential of you're just throwing money away and you have no protections. And what will happen, of course, there's inevitably going to be someone that says credit card fees are immoral. This system of protections and perks only exists because you have people that are paying cash into the system that are using debit cards in the system with no interchange fees at merchants that are now like essentially funding the credit card rewards because there's an interchange fee. This interchange fee the merchant is paying meaning the price of goods is being raised and it's being passed along to the consumer. And there is an argument to that. But I think my counterargument is the very DoddFrank Act that reduced the debit card interchange fees. There's no evidence that because of that law that the price of any goods were reduced. And this kind of tracks logically because many merchants, right, if they're selling an item for $2.99 and they want to have this nice pricing bucket, a 3% transaction fee makes it very weird to change that bucket. um they're not going to raise it to like $35 or something because of the interchange fee. And I would also argue there's a certain, you know, because of the protections associated with electronic payments. Yes, this is also true on debit cards, but because of the fact that like with credit cards, you can't have someone like shortchanging you at the register, no one stealing your cash, you can't be robbed as a as an inerson store. Uh there's a and you don't have to handle the cash. There's a certain operational cost savings that merchants are also benefiting from. And so the argument really I would make is that there's not a real piece of evidence that removing credit card interchange fees would in fact reduce the price of goods in really any major capacity because what would really just happen is merchants would likely just be pocketing the difference. Now, right, would you rather have the credit card company pocket the difference or the merchant? Obviously, like that's a different argument, right? Small stores totally get that. Like you want to have them, you know, be taking more of their actual revenue. But my main argument here is that credit cards are a much more powerful tool for so many people just because the fact that you have this extra layer of protection. And of course there is like one very very notable exception. Um you should never use a credit card for an ATM. That that's just stupid because you'll be paying cash advance fees. You'll be paying fees to the credit card company like instantaneously. A lot of times there will be no interest grace period. You'll be paying that upfront. Um you should be using a debit card for an ATM. And if you are using a debit card for an ATM, I highly recommend using one that doesn't charge ATM fees or at least refunds the fees. The one that I personally use that a lot of people recommend is the Charles Schwab debit card because that debit card refunds unlimited ATM fees globally anywhere at any ATM in the world. And that is really nice because now I can just go get cash and not have to pay any fee. Um because a lot of ATMs, right, they only have like, oh, if it's only within your network, it's really stupid. There's no like minimum balance for the Charles Schwab debit card. Um, I don't have a referral link. This is not like sponsored or anything, but I just highly recommend that card. Other than that though, in any case that you are able to use a credit card, you should be using a credit card. TITLE: The Huge Issue With Airbnb
VIDEO_ID: V6ETR3Gy4HU
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=V6ETR3Gy4HU
DATE: 2025-10-08
================================================================================

Many years ago, people thought Airbnbs would kill hotels. And simply put, that has not happened at all. And hotels are for sure here to stay. So today, I want to talk about why the business model of Airbnbs is fundamentally not a threat to hotels and why hotels will continue to persist. I want to start this out by saying I have nothing against Airbnb as a company or a business model. As someone who's been through Y Combinator, I've he speak. I think what they're doing is an amazing product and I think the company will be around for a very long time, but I think there are a few fundamental things that they might be missing in their strategy that I think would be interesting to discuss. And so, one of the fundamental aspects that I think Airbnb is totally missing is the lack of appeal to any business travelers. Obviously, this comes from someone who's like a points person and loves loyalty programs and all that, but simply put, Airbnb doesn't have a loyalty program. And the problem with this, right, the logic behind for Airbnb on this is why are we giving customers this rebate on things that don't really matter, right? Why don't we just make the product really good, make them want to come back? That's all great. One of the things though that really does benefit hotels with their loyalty programs is business travelers absolutely love them because what happens is it's a form of payment almost to the business traveler where my company pays for my travel to stay at this Marriott for 3 weeks a year whatever right maybe I'm a consultant maybe I'm traveling all the time I get those points I get those elite qualifying nights I get the status benefits and then when it's time to use my PTO and time to take my family on vacation I have now built up this huge points balance. So by me staying loyal to this hotel brand, I end up amassing this like big points balance because my company's paying for it and then I get to go on vacation with my family. And yes, there's an argument to like why are we not just taking the savings and passing it on to the company because the company doesn't really care. Um but it's something that for this for the psych psychology reasons of it, people really like it. People like receiving a rebate. It makes people pick a particular hotel brand irrationally and it makes people make irrational decisions. And I think that's something always really to remember in any type of business application is that people are not rational, right? People irrationally choose things all the time. And I think it's no different with hotels and loyalty programs and people make decisions that economically might not make sense but to them emotionally do make sense. And so I think if Airbnb were to implement some type of like a business travel or loyalty program, I think it would be really interesting. And I think there would be this potential shift of certain people wanting to stay in Airbnbs more often because they feel like they are benefiting something where they're actually receiving some type of rebate and it rewards them for continuing to come back. Because as it currently stands, there's no real reason for me after staying in 10 Airbnbs to keep staying with Airbnbs. There's no sunk cost fallacy. If I stay at a high-end hotel and I, you know, earn 40 EQNS, 50 EQNS, I'm just 10 EQN's away from Globalist, which provides me a whole suite of benefits. And look, that's going to cause people to make irrational decisions and want to book highs even when maybe it's not the most optimal decision because there's this sunk cost fallacy. But that really doesn't exist with Airbnb, you know, in the in the current state. And so I think it's I think it's costing them. I think another thing that is both the selling point and the downside of Airbnb is the lack of consistency. Hotel chains, love them or hate them, a lot of times they're very consistent. And this is what chains do very well. And this is something I, you know, I've heard Rory Rory Sutherland talk about this. And if you you don't know him, he's a brilliant advertising guy. But basically, chains for the most part are really good at raising the floor for something. Not so much the ceiling, but they're great at raising the floor. Think about what Starbucks has done for coffee. Look, Starbucks is not some super aspirational premium brand where every time I go, it's this like amazing mega experience, but it's pretty goddamn consistent and it's pretty reliable. And that's what they've done. And that's what a lot of budget hotel chains try to do, right? That's like High Place, uh, Holiday Inn, Courtyard. They try to be consistent as possible because they know when you're at these places, not, you know, where they're not that expensive and these hotels, you don't really know what you're going to get. Consistency is key. And look at high-end luxurious Airbnbs, right? You know, the consistency probably doesn't matter. Similar to luxury hotels, the consistency does not matter as much. But because of the fact Airbnbs are all unique, run by unique hosts, there's a lack of consistency. And for some people, obviously, this is going to be something that is really exciting to them. They get to try out new properties. They don't know what they're going to get exactly, and it's super unique, and that's great. But for people, for example, business travelers, they want something a little more consistent. They want to have a better understanding of what they're going to get when they're staying there, especially if you're doing travel for work for extended periods of time. And so, similar to the previous point, I think this is hurting them with business travelers. In that regard, I think really the only thing they could probably do about this is try to make certain soft products standardized within an Airbnb ecosystem, similar to what Vicassa does, where Vicassas, if you stay at them in certain markets, they will essentially be managed by Vicasa. And so they'll have like the same toiletries. You'll interact with like the same people. It's a very it's closer to a hotel experience. I would not call it a hotel experience, but it's much more in line with that and you have a much better idea of what you're getting. And that's one reason I've personally have enjoyed staying in the classes because I know there's a minimum service standard. When there have been issues, they've been quick to resolve them. One time I was in Hawaii, there was like the shower curtain was like pretty moldy. Honestly, not even like a big deal because it wasn't that much mold. I I sent him a text or a call or something. It was like kind of late. I remember next morning like 10:00 a.m. really nice representative was at my front door of the unit, brought like two different shower curtains for me to like pick between. I, you know, she offered to install it. I was like, "No, no worries. I got it." That was great. That was a great experience. And the fact that, you know, Vicasa has been able to achieve like a closer level of service consistency, I think is is really unique. And I think Airbnb if they could do that whether being like similar soft products right using the same towels, same beddings, same toiletries, having certain you know management operations be kind of centralized. I think that could be really interesting in certain business markets because I think it is hurting them in that regard because there is this unknown factor where I don't exactly know what I'm going to get. And in that vein, the next problem of course is with the lack of consistency is the problem of hosts increasingly turning this into what certain hotels are trying to do and kind of like I would say the equivalent of nickel and dimming the guest. And what I mean by this is asking a guest to clean things unnecessarily, right? Obviously, there's going to be someone doing housekeeping in between each guests. Um saying things like strip the bed, run a load of laundry, do all these chores. And look, I'm not saying that's unreasonable, per se, but if you're wanting to attract guests to have like a very easy experience, again, I I keep pushing on business travelers, but again, especially for business travelers, they don't want to do that crap. Okay, that's what's nice about a hotel. That's what so many people love about a hotel. You get to your hotel, you do what you need to do, you leave, you don't have to do anything. The towels are fresh, the bed is made, the trash is taken out. That is something that is really nice for a lot of people because it's consistent, right? And so I think for for leisure travelers, maybe for extended travel, not as big of a deal, right? Because okay, I don't need someone to make my bed every day. I don't need someone to take out the trash like every little tiny trash can in the room, right? That's fine. But again, for business travelers and for people doing like shorter stays, they don't want to deal with that. And I think this would also kind of be solved if we had if there was a more of a consistent system for host management and other things because again, people wouldn't be as worried about this. This has kind of become a common trope in people talking about Airbnbs, having these poor host experiences. And I think it's something that for a lot of people, myself included, do sometimes like turn me off about an Airbnb because I'm like, look, I'm don't want to do chores on vacation. I don't really want to deal with a inconsistent host. I don't really know what I'm getting. Yes, there's like ratings and all that, but the fact that there's no centralized way to resolve issues other than of course like Airbnb customer service, which is shaky to be fair, I think it's something that for a lot of people makes it unnecessarily difficult to kind of shift into the Airbnb ecosystem. And then of course there's like just more opaque ease with Airbnb. Obviously there are issues like the again no centralized service but I think one thing that's interesting is also the the the search fatigue and I think this is a a challenge in the business model where because each property is unique it makes it very tiresome and very tedious to search for Airbnbs in a market. This is somewhat helped by of course price filtering but in certain markets it becomes really annoying to search through specific Airbnb properties. And I think like for example, if I'm going to be searching in like a major city and I have a certain budget and the budget's fairly large, right? Like there's going to be a million Airbnbs I could pick between. But for hotels, if I have a, you know, I'm like, "Okay, I'm only going to filter by luxury brands. I want to be within these chains because I have these like loyalty programs." It's a much quicker search. And I think it's just like for the example, if you give someone, there's studies where you give someone like, let's say, put a bunch of jelly beans in front of them, okay? You give them two flavors of jelly beans, they pick one, they're going to be happy with their choice. You give them 50, they're not going to be happy with their choice. And I think the problem is like there's this search fatigue. Now, I don't really know how Airbnb would solve this. TITLE: Why Credit Card Annual Fees Are SO EXPENSIVE
VIDEO_ID: mveXEznpEOM
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mveXEznpEOM
DATE: 2025-10-09
================================================================================

It's no question that over the past few years, credit card annual fees have pretty much skyrocketed. And it seems like this trend is not slowing down at all. So, I want to talk about why this is happening, what I see the future of credit card annual fees holding, and kind of the overall way that you should be thinking about this going forward. It seems like even just a few years ago, the MX Platinum was 550, the Sapphire Reserve was $450. A lot of credit cards around the $400 to $500 mark on the premium segment. But recently, a lot of these cards have been pressing into like the eight to $900 segment, uh, with just a few stragglers remaining around the $400 to $500 mark. And we've seen this huge rise of coupon book credit cards, credit cards that have these, you know, coupon book of credits where you're getting, for example, the MX Platinum, Uber credit, hotel credit, streaming credits, a bunch of things that really aren't that practical for a lot of people. And we've seen other issuers adopt this type of strategy. The recent Sapphire Reserve refresh adopted this. The City Strata Elite adopted this. There's only a handful of cards that really haven't taken this playbook. And I predict that at some point they will. And there's a few reasons for this, but I think the main thing that's important to talk about and the most obvious is when MX is giving you an Uber credit or one of these banks is giving you a credit that goes to one or a small selection of third party merchants, the bank is not paying face value for that credit. Not even close. I don't know the exact economics of this, but they are either getting it at a ridiculous discount, nothing, or in fact being paid for it depending on kind of the prestige of the merchant and the value of the credit. And so what's really interesting about this is it does create this in theory a really good win-win situation where the banks charge you an annual fee, but now you're able to get outsized value from that annual fee via a bunch of these credits. And I think what's going to happen is banks are going to keep leveraging these because let's say you're a brand like Sachs or Uber or something that's, you know, kind of on a more premium segment brand, maybe Uber not, but some of these brands are definitely like in a premium segment. And you know that statistically people that hold MX Platinum cards as a general cohort are going to be wealthier, are going to spend more money, are going to be pretty good customers. And you want to reach that subset of customers because they've already been self-filtered. So, what you want to do is have a credit that ideally is useful enough for the people that hold that card to want to use, but not so useful to where you're spending a bunch of money on it. And so, I think like a great example of this is Sachs, where a lot of people go out of their way to use the Sax credit. Um, because it is like $50, however, right? Like most items at Sachs are going to cost more than $50. And so, what ends up happening is like you end up spending more money at that merchant. You become more accustomed to that merchant. And then what's going to happen is maybe there's certain things where, hey, I need a new item. Oh, you know, I bet Sachs might have it. And it's just this whole psychology of once you're familiar with something, you're more likely to keep using it and keep going back to it. And I think that's really the core component of like why these brands are willing to give these banks these credits and what's going on. And so I think what's going to keep happening is once MX kind of, you know, made this a sustainable model for them on the platinum card and banks took very clear notice of like, geez, the MX is doing really well, they customers still keep getting the card after they increase the annual fee. Wow, it's really not costing them that much because these credits are at merchants that are, you know, providing them the credits um at, you know, at the very least really, really good discounts. TITLE: The Ultimate Budget Airline (LCC) Hack

================================================================================

A lot of you don't want to fly lowcost carriers, myself included. But if you do find yourself flying a lowcost carrier, I have a little hack for you that you can exploit at your own risk because there's not there is a potential problem with this hack, but it also potentially can pay off. And I've done this before and it has paid off for me personally. So lowcost carriers of course require you to pay a stupid amount of money to select your seat. However, of course, they're going to assign you a seat at check-in. But if you are one of the last people to check in and the only seats are available are extra leg room seats or exit row seats, they will assign you one of those for free. And a lot of times they are open on these lowcost carriers even like very very close to the departure. And I mean like people literally like check in an hour to departure or less or like my friend literally like showed TSA his reservation confirmation. He's like, "Look, I'm not checked in yet, but I want to check in later." And they let him through. So you can check in literally like, you know, 15, 20 minutes of departure to be the last person to check into the flight. And if they only have exit row seats or extra leg room seats, they will give you one for free. And I did this on an easy jet flight before. And yes, we got like extra row seats for free. There was no one in the middle. It was really great. We actually was like, "Wow, this is one of the nicest flights I've taken domestically within Europe and on one of these carriers." Because, yeah, I was expecting it to be like a really shitty flight, but it was great because exit lo exit row seats, extra leg room, no one in the middle. It was awesome and we didn't have to pay anything extra for it. So, the downside of this though, of course, is let's say you were willing to pick your seat and pay for it or something like that, or maybe they have plenty of seats open and they're not extra leg room seats. You could of course get screwed and be in some middle in the back. So do it at your own risk, but I know a friend that does this a lot and he kind of swears by it and I have tried it before and it works. So it's a nice little lowcost carrier hack for you to make a lowcost carrier trip a little bit more pleasant if you can get one of those seats. Uh, this is something that I'm hoping doesn't get shared around too widely because we don't want to have everyone sitting there trying to check in last to get these seats and then it becomes a it becomes a game of you're just playing a game of chicken at this point and it's kind of stupid. Essentially, in Las Vegas, they are actually very keen to upgrade rooms if you're willing to combine multiple rooms. So, let's say you have two base rooms booked and you email the hotel, call the hotel, speak to someone, ask them to combine these rooms, maybe into a two-bedroom suite or more consistently into a large, very nice one-bedroom suite. It's something that they're actually very open to doing in the Las Vegas market, obviously, depending on the hotel, because a lot of times they oversell rooms. They don't always sell the high tier suites. they would like to be able to sell more base rooms because those are easier to sell. So, sometimes it can actually work very nicely. Uh there can be an upgrade charge sometimes, but oftentimes there also may not be an upgrade charge. This is applicable for a few reasons. one, the suites you are upgrading into are almost always lower in price or sorry, almost always higher in priced than the actual two base rooms you'd be buying to combine it into that suite. So, it's a way to save money on higher suites. But much more interestingly, if you are someone that has watched the previous video I made about buying FHR credits on Churning Marketplace or other similar affiliated marketplaces, then it is very interesting because what you can do is you can buy these credits and then use them to combine really really cheap rooms into very high tier rooms. Because if you're buying these credits at $60, $70 or something, then what you can do is get a really really big suite for super cheap and you don't pay a second resort fee usually. So, let's say you buy two rooms, your cost per room is $60 base. Yes, you have to pay a $60 resort fee per room, but because you're combining into one room, that second resort fee often could be knocked off. I don't know if you get to keep both of the MX credits or not, the FHR credits, but we're talking, at least some of the examples this lady was giving me are pretty ridiculous where we're talking like $1,800 night suites for the actual out-of- pocket cost of like $180 to $240. There are certain hotels I know this does not work at. Uh, for example, I spoke with the front desk at the win and it didn't seem like they were too keen on doing it. But I have heard data points of certain Vegas hotels, some of the large casino hotels, not the wind, but other ones that are pretty nice that have a willingness to do this. So, this is a very interesting hack. If you're going to Vegas, maybe consider, especially if you have a lot of FHR credits or things to burn on cheap rooms, see if you can contact the hotel, say, "Hey, I'm willing to book two, three, four base rooms. What can you give me if we combine them all?" Often times, they will give you a two-bedroom suite. Especially if you're willing to combine, let's say, three base rooms into a two-bedroom suite. they might be very very keen on doing that and it's a way you can act like a really big baller in Vegas in a very very very expensive suite for a vastly vastly lower cost. TITLE: Hyatt's Secret Top Tier Status (Above Globalist)
VIDEO_ID: 3qF-OqSr5UM

DATE: 2025-11-12
================================================================================

If you think Hyatt Globalist is their top tier status, you are wrong. Let me clarify. Hiatt of course publicly has three tiers of elite status. There's discoverist, explorist, and then globalist at the top. And so many people think that when you reach globalist, you are the top tier of high elite chain. This is wrong for two reasons. There's actually two tiers higher. So the first tier that actually is higher and it may sound like it's not but it's actually the same or it's actually higher is lifetime globalist. Lifetime globalist is essentially a globalist that has never going to have it expire. You have to spend $200,000 USD with Hyatt on your account pre-tax and prefees to earn the status. This does actually rank you differently than a normal globalist on their upgrade tier list on the hotel when they're when they're checking you in beforehand. When they're blocking the rooms before your stay, they can see who's a lifetime globalist and who isn't. And anecdotally, they absolutely absolutely positively prioritize lifetime globalist over globalist. It only makes sense. someone that has spent $200,000 in their lifetime with the chain over someone that's spending 20 nights earning a status challenge, they're going to prioritize the person spending a lot of money with the chain. That's a given. The other tier, of course, is courtesy card. For those of you not familiar, courtesy card is arguably the best hotel status in the world. The only one that would compete is Shangrila Polaris, but that's a topic for a different time. courtesy card is not like globalist. There is no publicly earning criteria. There is really a few ways to get it. The e the easiest quote unquote is to own a high hotel. By easiest I mean it is the most direct. If you own a high hotel you're probably getting courtesy card especially if you are the sole owner. Like you basically are just getting it. TITLE: The Scammers Ruining Credit Card Rewards
VIDEO_ID: uTRomOrCciY
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uTRomOrCciY
DATE: 2025-12-19
================================================================================

There's a world of people that are essentially points brokers that are costing the airlines a lot of money and are messing up award release patterns for all of us. Essentially, there are these people that will go out and buy up the entire airline premium cabin with various credit cards and names and fake accounts and all of this. And then what they will do is close into departure or maybe within a week or two to departure will cancel dozens of seats, first class, business class, whatever cabin they're trying to optimize for. And what they will, the reason they do this is because these airlines usually, especially if they have a fairly consistent award release pattern, relative to the number of seats that are open for sale on the aircraft, they know they will be able to consistently book award seats. So, let's say you're a points brokerage firm. Now, this is illegal to be clear. I'm not advocating for this at all. I'm making this you aware of this. You're a points brokerage firm and you want to sell your client a business class ticket somewhere and you want to sell it for very very cheap. The problem is you need award space to be able to sell it for cheap and there's no award space open and but your client wants to go on a very specific date and you want to be able to guarantee them that you can get them a seat on this date. The problem is if you just wait for the award space to open it may or may not open and this is a problem. However, if you let's say buy 20 cash tickets on the cabin in business class and then you a week two from departure, you cancel all of those tickets because they're fully refundable. Now, the airline has a lot of seats for sale, very very close into departure, and they're like, "Oh my god, we're not going to sell these seats." So, it goes into the award inventory system and then you can book them a seat. This is very profitable for these points brokers because they are able to essentially without any risk or not much cost at least hold tons of airline seats and get their clients consistently cheap business cost bookings which of course they're charging probably a hefty markup for still. This is very very legal and obviously against all airline terms and services. And this is very bad for us as award travelers and enthusiasts because what happens is it causes airlines to lose consistent award release patterns. If a airline consistently releases close-in award seats and they are being attacked essentially by these points brokerage firms that are booking up entire cabins on refundable tickets and then canceling these tickets because they know they have a consistent award release pattern. the airline is going to get rid of their consistent award release pattern because then it messes up these points brokerages because then they there's not really the same profitability of booking out the cabin and canceling it because then they still can't guarantee the client getting a seat. That's why you will see airlines fly out cabins with empty first classes or semi- empty first classes or half empty business classes without releasing extra award space. It's because at least in part these points brokerages are messing it up for everyone. There's not too much we can do here except really just appreciate the airlines that do have consistent close-in award release patterns because the ones that do hopefully aren't being attacked by these points brokerage firms, at least the sketchier ones. It is an issue in the space because when there's a good thing, people try to exploit the good thing and that's not necessarily a problem. But when it starts to be exploited on a commercial and almost enterprise level scale, the airlines have no choice but to get rid of the good thing because it's not acting as a subsidy to their own consumers. It is acting as a subsidy to some other private enterprise that is trying to essentially scam the airline. Which is why we've seen a lot of airlines like Japan Airlines for example not really have close and award release patterns anymore. used to be very consistent to book and book close in jou first class. It's still not impossible. It still definitely opens up, but not nearly as consistently as it used to a while back. And I think part of the reason was that people were just buying out the first class cabin. They would be points brokers buying eight first class tickets on refundable tickets and then just canceling them close in and then oh look at this a bunch of award seats open. Look, I got my client a bunch of jal f tickets. I was able to get their whole family an F, for example. You know, people are people are doing this and it's not good. It's very very bad. So, be aware of this. Not really too much you can do about it, but I thought it'd be an interesting video to just make you all aware of of this issue and what's going on and why some airlines have been having worse award release patterns.

TITLE: The Secret Tactic to Get Better Upgrades at Hotels

There's a method of dealing with hotels which I have personally found a decent amount of success with and I found it more likely the case that I can consistently get better upgrades and I guess like better perks out of hotels by doing this. If you're going to book the second room, right, make sure that you're going to actually do it. And don't be shocked if it doesn't work because there's going to be cases when it doesn't work. But I found it to be a higher percentage of success rather than just asking for something directly if that makes sense. If I'm trying to get something usually this isn't like if I'm trying to get something beyond what is the standard terms if that makes sense. If you want something a little beyond the standard terms, I've had a significantly higher success rate with this strategy than just booking and and doing it up front because when the hotel is able to get extra business from you, they want to do an extra step if they're going to be able to get that extra business. This does not work if you are going to book a hotel significantly like far in advance and say like, "Hey, can you upgrade me if I book?" That doesn't work. Okay? TITLE: Why Hotels Upgrade NON-Status People MORE?

DATE: 2025-12-19
================================================================================

Believe it or not, some hotels, if they need to upgrade someone from a low tier room to a very high tier suite, will actually pick a non-status member to do this with. I may have talked about this before, but a hotel, let's say, is oversold. They need to upgrade someone. They have a presidential suite open. They need to give it to someone because the hotel's oversold. It's a high property in this example. Let's say they they logically should give it to a globalist, right? But they may not. There are properties that will actively give these upgrades rather to a no status member. Why might this be? From a logical perspective, Ray, it makes no sense. But taking the logic out of it for a second in terms of a practical consequence of the hotel, you're a globalist member who gets upgraded to a presidential suite at a property. This you're a globalist member, so you travel a lot. You stay in a lot of Hayatts. You probably talk on the Hyatt forums and talk to other Hyatt members. You tell all your Hyatt friends, hey, this property upgraded me to the presidential suite. Now they have a bunch of other globalists going to this hotel trying to get the same upgrade because they think it is an upgrade in the realm of possibility. Whereas if you just take a normal random member and give it to them who's never really stays at Hyatt, they're going, "Oh my god, this is this was great. I loved this hotel." They know it was totally out of the blue. They know they have no status. They know they don't do a lot of business hotel. They know they just got really lucky and now they've had this really really amazing experience and not expecting it again. It's almost like it's interesting because it's kind of the a like game theory example of why you could I'm not saying hotels ever should do this, but I'm saying like let's say you're this hotel trying to purely maximize like revenue or just your members or whatever. If you upgrade people without status, you don't have their business yet. So maybe by upgrading them you can capture their business. Whereas the status people, you already have their business. It's it's similar to how some hotels will actually choose to upgrade people that complain more rather than people that are nicer. Because if you have only so many upgrades to give and you know that these guests have a history of very strongly complaining if they don't get an upgrade versus these other guests who are totally chill, same status levels. If you upgrade the people that are totally chill, now you have people that were totally chill and now a little bit happier and a group of people who are kind of pissed off. Whereas, if you upgrade the people that were pissed off all the time, now you have a group of happier people and then also super chill people who didn't care either way that much. So, there are hotels that will just straight upgrade people that complain the most when they don't get upgraded. I've I've I've heard this story from a hotel manager before where they don't look at the amount of money you spend at the hotel, the amount of nights you have, anything like that. They they just are looking at who are the people that if they don't get upgraded have a record of like escalating it to customer service and getting really pissed off. They will upgrade those people sometimes. Don't start complaining just to get better upgrades, okay? I'm not, this is not the takeaway from this video, but it is interesting to be aware of how hotels will sometimes decide to upgrade people. And the point I'm trying to make is it's not always purely a factor of status. That is a huge factor in it. Status is a huge factor, but there are other factors sometimes that go into whether or not you get the upgrade. What I will say is I think the takeaway is always make it very very clear to the hotel that you value the upgrade. That's why I always ask for an upgrade of my reservations and I always ask for a specific room type because I've I haven't taken it to the level of complaining, but I have done enough to go, hey, this matters to me. So, if you have two equal status guests, one asks for the upgrade, one doesn't. Well, the hotel and from their perspective in this game theory perspective is going, "Well, I can give it to the person that asked because I know if I don't give it to them, they'll probably be a little disappointed because they asked for this upgrade." They'll probably prioritize the people that asked for it, but people that made it clear to them what they want. Always be clear in your communication. Make it very clear what you want and you are more likely to get it.

Are we in a credit card recession? And if so, how long is it going to last? And what's going to happen going forward? So, we have objectively seen over the past year and a half or so, a lot of credit cards get a lot worse. We're talking, you know, increases in annual fees. What's going on here? Why is it the case that we're seeing so many credit cards increase their annual fees? We're seeing so many benefits get cut. We're seeing approvals get tougher to get. We're seeing more and more banks implement restrictions on their signup bonuses. Why is this happening and what's going to happen going forward? So, there's a variety of factors here I want to talk about. One very clearly, probably the most obvious one a lot of people are going to talk about is the fact that interest rates are high. People are borrowing a lot still. Delinquencies have been on the rise, especially like in the subprime segment. And this is all true. Travel over the past few years has gotten disproportionately more expensive, especially like in and around the United States. It's actually not like as bad of, you know, points inflation as people would think when you have hotels that have to 2x their cash rate, you know, when they have to, let's say, 2x their points rate or 1.5x their points rate or whatever, it's actually like not totally unreasonable when you look at it from that end. And so what's going to keep happening I think is as we see these increase of demand persist in the luxury segment I think we will see more and more cards cut benefits and so I think there's kind of a few ways you can handle it and a ways to kind of defend yourself from it. Because Hilton claims, "Oh, we're dynamically priced." They're not really dynamically priced. It's just something they like to say. And so, like, by sticking with programs and diversifying yourself into programs that have this consumer-friendly angle to them, you are in a sense slightly more protected against points inflation. Because if Hyatt decides, hey, we're going to like totally raise the rates on all our points, but they give me a month's notice. Okay, great. I've like earned a bunch of points. When is a luxury hotel not really a luxury hotel? Today we're going to talk about a very unique category of properties as it pertains to credit card points and miles called points farm hotels. And the article discusses these hotels that are supposedly expensive and luxury but don't really like meet the high quality level of service expected of those properties and they're able to get away with it because many people who stay there are redeeming points for that hotel instead of paying cash. So Sean, why don't you elaborate a little more about this article and argument? >> Yes. So I think we should start with how hotels are compensated for point stays because this is a very very important thing to understand. So at low levels of occupancy what happens is chains will compensate an individual hotel at a pretty measily rate maybe related a bit to the daily average rate of the hotel but various a small percentage. Now when occupancy gets pretty high we're talking like 90 95% 100%. What will happen is for those point stays. considered that like okay these points guests because let's say you're at 100% occupancy they did likely take away a paying guest from the hotel at least from the cor the corporate view is that and so the corporate chain will then reimburse the hotel at nearly the full rate of what their average daily rate is for that property. And this creates a very interesting economic incentive for a hotel because if a hotel has very high cash rates, that means that those point stays are paying them more money. But it's also a weird cycle because the higher the cash prices of a hotel, the more points people it attracts because they're getting a better deal. And if a hotel is able to fill a lot of their rooms with points people, now they can boost their occupancy. So now they can set artificially high cash rates which attract a bunch of points people which increase the occupancy percentage and then overall the compensation goes up for the hotel. And so it's a very interesting incentive for a lot of hotels to do this because when they get all these points people it's just free money. And because they know they have these points people who aren't really paying the retail cost, they know their standards are significantly lower than what someone paying that retail cost is. So, you might have a hotel that would normally go for, let's say, $600 a night, but because they, let's say, want to get more points people and also want to get more compensation from the corporate chain, they may raise their rates to $800 a night or $900 a night, which in turn attracts more points people, which helps fills up the hotel. Yes, they're getting less people paying cash because the prices are higher, but they don't care. They're getting more rooms filled, getting more overall compensation this way. And the problem with this is the people who are paying that retail rate of 800 or 900 a night are in no other words being ripped off. TITLE: Travel Portals Are Stupid.
VIDEO_ID: c1m_57Su-3k
URL: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=c1m_57Su-3k
DATE: 2026-01-25
================================================================================

Today we have a very important message to deliver. You should never use a travel portal in almost any circumstance. They are a scam. Today we're going to talk about it. My name is Sean and I'm here with my co-host. >> Hi everyone. I'm Sherwin. On today's episode of the credit card to BS podcast, we're going to talk about why travel portals are mostly a scam. So many credit cards will offer you great multipliers for booking travel through their portal. 10x on hotels, rental cars, seen 8x, I've seen 5x for booking flights. Now, does this mean it's a better deal to book through them rather than booking directly since you're getting a higher rebate from your credit card? Now, here's the problem. You're almost always worse off booking your travel through a travel portal rather than directly with the provider despite these higher multipliers. And why, Sean? Why don't we get into that immediately? So, it's a fundamental problem with the way online travel agencies and travel portals operate as a business. Essentially, they are this like weird form of payment processing where what happens is there are aggregators, right? So, for people to book travel, they usually just go to their favorite travel site cuz it has everything on there. But what happens is these travel aggregators go, "Hey, hotel, we give you a ton of business and because we give you a ton of business, we earn a huge commission or we deserve a huge commission." And so they end up taking 20 30% of the hotel rate off the top that the hotel isn't getting. They take a smaller percentage for airline rates. And what happens is right, their argument is like we're providing this big service, but they're not. The only reason they're providing this big service is because they're so ubiquitous and because since everyone expects every travel portal to have every hotel, they all need to have every hotel and every hotel needs to be on all these travel portals because otherwise they lose a huge amount of business. And so a huge portion of the rate ends up going to these, you know, third parties. And what happens is the hotels and the airlines, well mainly the hotels go, "Well, this rate because you're paying this stupid commission to this travel agency and it's like you're on the service paying the same rate, but we're getting a whole lot less of it. We're going to treat you much less much worse than most of our other members, right?" And what happens is is now you also have this middleman you have to deal with for when things go wrong. You're you missed your flight, you want to change your flight, things like that. it just becomes more of a headache because you now instead of contacting the travel provider directly in this case the airline or the hotel you now need to contact this weird middleman who then needs to contact them on your behalf. Now in some cases in some weird cases like yes maybe it can be fine and it could you know work out but in most cases it's like better oh I have an issue with the hotel let me just talk to the hotel chain let me just talk to the hotel where you know you'll be much better off if you just book direct. Yeah. And you know, like hotels often just like treat 30 third third party bookings with like a lot of annoyance because of the extra fees and commissions you're giving out. So they'll just kind of give you the minimal amount of service compared to if you're booking direct lowest party. Like if a member books directly on a particular type of room and like a non-member uses one of these portals which you know is treated like a hotel.com or a kayak booking a third party booking like they'll still give you like the worst room. Like even a standard room is not created equal. Like the number of times I've been assigned to a second floor room facing the dumpster in the back is like when I've booked through one of these portals is like so much higher and like it almost never happens when I book direct. But like when I use a portal it's considered lower priority. Also you even if you have like elite status with the hotel and you put in your number that's not going to be recognized like you're treated as if you had no status at all. you're not going to be able to earn elite night credits toward status if you're using a third-party booking. Even if you're booking with like Chase or City or Capital One, right? It's considered third party. Now, there are exceptions with like flights where sometimes it can work, but hotels generally they don't make any exceptions for this. And in most cases, like the extra points earning from the travel portal would have just been earned by like booking through the hotel program directly because they do give you points on your spend there. And what's really interesting actually is it's this. actually flipped where these type of bookings are often considered more premium and a higher priority than hotels booked direct or like bookings directly with the hotel where if I go book let's say to a park hyatt and I book a standard rate versus you know through high directly and I'm a globalist versus I go on MX FHR and book it and I attach my Hayatt globalist number the FHR booking will actually in most cases be considered a higher level booking and thus more important for like a higher room upgrade. But of course, the real message here is don't book on some like random portal like don't use MX Travel, don't use Expedia, don't use Booking.com because none of those will provide you, you know, the the same priority of how important your rate is. >> Yeah. Who wants to stay in a standard room at a hotel? I certainly don't. So, today we're going to talk about hotel confirmable upgrade awards. Ways that you can get a room upgrade on your next hotel stay potentially to just a semi- higher tier room or a very, very nice suite. Those usually operate in addition to and on top of these confirmable upgrade awards. Um but we're talking about specific concrete upgrade instruments that are given to you as part of the loyalty program that you can apply in advance to a stay and sort of get an upgrade ahead of time and of higher priority and certainty. Uh you can select two sweet upgrade awards at 50 elite nights. You will get two sweet upgrade awards. At 60 EQNS and beginning from 70 nights, 80 90 all the way to 150.
